This requires a fundamental rethinking of the global economic architecture, and a renewed commitment to social justice, human rights, and environmental sustainability.\n\nIn order to achieve this, several key steps are necessary. Firstly, there is a need for greater regulation and oversight of trans-national corporations, to ensure that they operate in a socially and environmentally responsible manner. This includes measures to prevent tax avoidance, to protect workers\' rights, and to promote sustainable development.\n\nSecondly, there is a need for greater investment in education, healthcare, and infrastructure, to ensure that the benefits of globalisation are shared more equitably. This includes measures to promote access to education and healthcare, to reduce inequality, and to promote social mobility.\n\nThirdly, there is a need for greater international cooperation and solidarity, to ensure that the benefits of globalisation are shared more widely, and that the negative impacts are mitigated. This inequality is a result of the policies and institutions that have been put in place to facilitate globalisation, which have often favoured the interests of corporations over those of people and communities.\n\nThirdly, the principle of serving humanity should guide our approach to economic globalisation. Therefore, it is crucial that economic globalization is regulated in a way that prioritizes the needs and well-being of people over the profits of corporations. This can be achieved through policies that promote fair trade, protect workers' rights, and ensure that the benefits of globalization are shared more equitably. Ultimately, economic globalization should be seen as a tool for promoting human development, rather than as an end in itself."}]
